Click here for more information." data-file-height="438" data-file-width="462" decoding="async" height="19" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e7/Cscr-featured.svg/20px-Cscr-featured.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e7/Cscr-featured.svg/30px-Cscr-featured.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e7/Cscr-featured.svg/40px-Cscr-featured.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></div>\n</div>\n<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Photon</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">\n<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>\n<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Particle of light</div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the elementary particle of light. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Photon_(disambiguation)" title="Photon (disambiguation)">Photon (disambiguation)</a>.</div>\n<table class="infobox" style="width:22em"><caption>Photon</caption><tbody><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Particle#Composition" title="Particle">Composition</a></th><td><a href="/wiki/Elementary_particle" href="#cite_note-Particle_table_2009-1">[1]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">Electric&#160;charge</a></th><td>0\n<br/><span class="nowrap">&lt; <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6946160217648700000&#9824;</span>1<span id="cite_ref-Particle_table_2009_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-Particle_table_2009-1">[1]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Spin_(physics)" title="Spin (physics)">Spin</a></th><td>1</td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Parity_(physics)" title="Parity (physics)">Parity</a></th><td>&#8722;1<sup class="reference" title="C-parity">C&#160;parity</a></th><td>&#8722;1<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Particle_table_2009_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-Particle_table_2009-1">[1]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Condensed</th><td><i><a href="/wiki/Weak_isospin" title="Weak isospin">I</a></i>(<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Total_angular_momentum" title="Total angular momentum">J</a></i><sup><i><a href="/wiki/Parity_(physics)" title="Parity (physics)">P</a> <a href="/wiki/C_parity" title="C parity">C</a></i></sup>)=0,1(1<sup>&#8722;&#8722;</sup>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Particle_table_2009_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-Particle_table_2009-1">[1]</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>photon</b> is a type of <a href="/wiki/Elementary_particle" title="Elementary particle">elementary particle</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Quantum" title="Quantum">quantum</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_field" title="Electromagnetic field">electromagnetic field</a> including <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic radiation</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">light</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Force_carrier" title="Force carrier">force carrier</a> for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_force" title="Electromagnetic force">electromagnetic force</a> (even when <a href="/wiki/Static_forces_and_virtual-particle_exchange" title="Static forces and virtual-particle exchange">static</a> via <a href="/wiki/Virtual_particle" title="Virtual particle">virtual particles</a>). The photon has zero <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rest_mass" title="Rest mass">rest mass</a> and always moves at the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a> within a <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">vacuum</a>.\n</p><p>Like all elementary particles, photons are currently best explained by <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a> and exhibit <a href="/wiki/Wave%E2%80%93particle_duality" title="Wave&#8211;particle duality">wave&#8211;particle duality</a>, exhibiting properties of both <a href="/wiki/Wave" title="Wave">waves</a> and <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/particle" title="wikt:particle">particles</a>. For example, a single photon may be <a href="/wiki/Refraction" title="Refraction">refracted</a> by a <a href="/wiki/Lens_(optics)" title="Lens (optics)">lens</a> and exhibit <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interference_(wave_propagation)" title="Interference (wave propagation)">wave interference</a> with itself, and it can behave as a particle with definite and finite measurable position or <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">momentum</a>, though not both at the same time as per the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heisenberg%27s_uncertainty_principle" title="Heisenberg\'s uncertainty principle">Heisenberg\'s uncertainty principle</a>.  The photon\'s wave and quantum qualities are two observable aspects of a single phenomenon&#8212;they cannot be described by any mechanical model;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> a representation of this dual property of light that assumes certain points on the wavefront to be the seat of the energy is not possible. The quanta in a light wave are not spatially localized.\n</p><p>The modern concept of the photon was developed gradually by <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> in the early 20th century to explain experimental observations that did not fit the classical <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_wave_equation" title="Electromagnetic wave equation">wave model</a> of light. The benefit of the photon model is that it accounts for the frequency dependence of light\'s energy, and explains the ability of <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a> and electromagnetic radiation to be in <a href="/wiki/Thermal_equilibrium" title="Thermal equilibrium">thermal equilibrium</a>. The photon model accounts for anomalous observations, including the properties of <a href="/wiki/Black-body_radiation" title="Black-body radiation">black-body radiation</a>, that others (notably <a href="/wiki/Max_Planck" title="Max Planck">Max Planck</a>) had tried to explain using <i>semiclassical models</i>. In that model, light is described by <a href="/wiki/Maxwell%27s_equations" title="Maxwell\'s equations">Maxwell\'s equations</a>, but material objects emit and absorb light in <i>quantized</i> amounts (i.e., they change energy only by certain particular discrete amounts). Although these semiclassical models contributed to the development of quantum mechanics, many further experiments<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> beginning with the phenomenon of <a href="/wiki/Compton_scattering" title="Compton scattering">Compton scattering</a> of single photons by electrons, validated Einstein\'s hypothesis that <i>light itself</i> is <a href="/wiki/Quantization_(physics)" title="Quantization (physics)">quantized</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-compton-lecture_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-compton-lecture-5">[5]</a></sup> In 1926 the optical physicist Frithiof Wolfers and the chemist <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_N._Lewis" title="Gilbert N. Lewis">Gilbert N. Lewis</a> coined the name "photon" for these particles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kragh_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-kragh-6">[6]</a></sup>  After Arthur H. Compton won the Nobel Prize in 1927 for his scattering studies,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>  most scientists accepted that light quanta have an independent existence, and the term "photon" was accepted.\n</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Standard_Model" title="Standard Model">Standard Model</a> of <a href="/wiki/Particle_physics" title="Particle physics">particle physics</a>, photons and other elementary particles are described as a necessary consequence of physical laws having a certain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symmetry_in_physics" title="Symmetry in physics">symmetry</a> at every point in <a href="/wiki/Spacetime" title="Spacetime">spacetime</a>. The intrinsic properties of particles, such as <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">charge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Invariant_mass" title="Invariant mass">mass</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Spin_(physics)" title="Spin (physics)">spin</a>, are determined by this <a href="/wiki/Gauge_theory" title="Gauge theory">gauge symmetry</a>.  The photon concept has led to momentous advances in experimental and theoretical physics, including <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">lasers</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bose%E2%80%93Einstein_condensation" title="Bose&#8211;Einstein condensation">Bose&#8211;Einstein condensation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quantum_field_theory" title="Quantum field theory">quantum field theory</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Probability_amplitude" title="Probability amplitude">probabilistic interpretation</a> of quantum mechanics. It has been applied to <a href="/wiki/Photochemistry" title="Photochemistry">photochemistry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Two-photon_excitation_microscopy" title="Two-photon excitation microscopy">high-resolution microscopy</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fluorescence_resonance_energy_transfer" title="Fluorescence resonance energy transfer">measurements of molecular distances</a>. Recently, photons have been studied as elements of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quantum_computer" title="Quantum computer">quantum computers</a>, and for applications in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optical_imaging" title="Optical imaging">optical imaging</a> and <a href="/wiki/Optical_communication" title="Optical communication">optical communication</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Quantum_cryptography" title="Quantum cryptography">quantum cryptography</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">20</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nomenclature">Nomenclature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Nomenclature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The word <a href="/wiki/Quantum" title="Quantum"><i>quanta</i></a> (singular <i>quantum,</i> Latin for <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/quantum" title="wikt:quantum">how much</a></i>) was used before 1900 to mean particles or amounts of different <a href="/wiki/Quantity" title="Quantity">quantities</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electricity</a>. In 1900, the German physicist <a href="/wiki/Max_Planck" title="Max Planck">Max Planck</a> was studying black-body radiation: he suggested that the experimental observations would be explained if the energy carried by electromagnetic <a href="/wiki/Wave" title="Wave">waves</a> could only be released in "packets" of energy. In his 1901 article <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Planck1901_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Planck1901-8">[8]</a></sup> in <i><a href="/wiki/Annalen_der_Physik" title="Annalen der Physik">Annalen der Physik</a></i> he called these packets "energy elements". In 1905, <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> published a paper in which he proposed that many light-related phenomena&#8212;including black-body radiation and the <a href="/wiki/Photoelectric_effect" title="Photoelectric effect">photoelectric effect</a>&#8212;would be better explained by modelling electromagnetic waves as consisting of spatially localized, discrete wave-packets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Einstein1905_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Einstein1905-9">[9]</a></sup> He called such a wave-packet <i>the light quantum</i> (German: <i>das Lichtquant</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[a]</a></sup> The name <i>photon</i> derives from the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek word</a> for light, <i><span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">&#966;&#8182;&#962;</span></i> (transliterated <i>ph&#244;s</i>). <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Compton" title="Arthur Compton">Arthur Compton</a> used <i>photon</i> in 1928, referring to <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_N._Lewis" title="Gilbert N. Lewis">Gilbert N. Lewis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup> The same name was used earlier, by the American physicist and psychologist <a href="/wiki/Leonard_T._Troland" title="Leonard T. Troland">Leonard T. Troland</a>, who coined the word in 1916, in 1921 by the Irish physicist <a href="/wiki/John_Joly" title="John Joly">John Joly</a>, in 1924 by the French physiologist <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Wurmser&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ren&#233; Wurmser (page does not exist)">Ren&#233; Wurmser</a> (1890&#8211;1993) and in 1926 by the French physicist <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Frithiof_Wolfers&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Frithiof Wolfers (page does not exist)">Frithiof Wolfers</a> (1891&#8211;1971).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kragh_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-kragh-6">[6]</a></sup> The name was suggested initially as a unit related to the illumination of the eye and the resulting sensation of light and was used later in a physiological context. Although Wolfers\'s and Lewis\'s theories were contradicted by many experiments and never accepted, the new name was adopted very soon by most physicists after Compton used it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kragh_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-kragh-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[b]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In physics, a photon is usually denoted by the symbol <i>&#947;</i> (the <a href="/wiki/Greek_alphabet" title="Greek alphabet">Greek letter</a> <a href="/wiki/Gamma" title="Gamma">gamma</a>). This symbol for the photon probably derives from <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma rays</a>, which were discovered in 1900 by <a href="/wiki/Paul_Ulrich_Villard" title="Paul Ulrich Villard">Paul Villard</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[13]</a></sup> named by <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Rutherford" title="Ernest Rutherford">Ernest Rutherford</a> in 1903, and shown to be a form of <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic radiation</a> in 1914 by Rutherford and <a href="/wiki/Edward_Andrade" title="Edward Andrade">Edward Andrade</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[14]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Optical_engineering" title="Optical engineering">optical engineering</a>, photons are usually symbolized by <i>h&#957;</i>, which is the <a href="/wiki/Photon_energy" title="Photon energy">photon energy</a>, where <i>h</i> is <a href="/wiki/Planck_constant" title="Planck constant">Planck constant</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Greek_alphabet" title="Greek alphabet">Greek letter</a> <i>&#957;</i> (<a href="/wiki/Nu_(letter)" title="Nu (letter)">nu</a>) is the photon\'s <a href="/wiki/Frequency" title="Frequency">frequency</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Liddle2015_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Liddle2015-17">[15]</a></sup> Much less commonly, the photon can be symbolized by <i>hf</i>, where its frequency is denoted by <i>f</i>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_properties">Physical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Physical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Special_relativity" title="Special relativity">Special relativity</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Light_cone_colour.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="515" data-file-width="333" decoding="async" height="340" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Light_cone_colour.svg/220px-Light_cone_colour.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Light_cone_colour.svg/330px-Light_cone_colour.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Light_cone_colour.svg/440px-Light_cone_colour.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Light_cone_colour.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The cone shows possible values of wave 4-vector of a photon. The "time" axis gives the angular frequency (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radians_per_second" title="Radians per second">rad&#8901;s<sup>&#8722;1</sup></a>) and the "space" axis represents the angular wavenumber (rad&#8901;m<sup>&#8722;1</sup>). Green and indigo represent left and right polarization</div></div></div>\n<p>A photon is <a href="/wiki/Massless_particle" title="Massless particle">massless</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[c]</a></sup> has no <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">electric charge</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chargeless_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-chargeless-20">[17]</a></sup> and is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stable_particle" title="Stable particle">stable particle</a>. A photon has two possible <a href="/wiki/Photon_polarization" title="Photon polarization">polarization</a> states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwartz2014_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schwartz2014-21">[18]</a></sup>  In the <a href="/wiki/Position_and_momentum_space" title="Position and momentum space">momentum representation</a> of the photon, which is preferred in quantum field theory, a photon is described by its <a href="/wiki/Wave_vector" title="Wave vector">wave vector</a>, which determines its wavelength <i>&#955;</i> and its direction of propagation. A photon\'s wave vector may not be zero and can be represented either as a <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_vector" title="Euclidean vector">spatial 3-vector</a> or as a (relativistic) <a href="/wiki/Four-vector" title="Four-vector">four-vector</a>; in the latter case it belongs to the <a href="/wiki/Light_cone" title="Light cone">light cone</a> (pictured). Different signs of the four-vector denote different <a href="/wiki/Circular_polarization" title="Circular polarization">circular polarizations</a>, but in the 3-vector representation one should account for the polarization state separately; it actually is a <a href="/wiki/Spin_quantum_number" title="Spin quantum number">spin quantum number</a>. In both cases the space of possible wave vectors is three-dimensional.\n</p><p>The photon is the <a href="/wiki/Gauge_boson" title="Gauge boson">gauge boson</a> for <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetism" title="Electromagnetism">electromagnetism</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>29&#8211;30</span></sup> and therefore all other quantum numbers of the photon (such as <a href="/wiki/Lepton_number" title="Lepton number">lepton number</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baryon_number" title="Baryon number">baryon number</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flavor_(particle_physics)#Flavour_quantum_numbers" title="Flavor (particle physics)">flavour quantum numbers</a>) are zero.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[20]</a></sup> Also, the photon does not obey the <a href="/wiki/Pauli_exclusion_principle" title="Pauli exclusion principle">Pauli exclusion principle</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Halliday_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Halliday-24">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>1221</span></sup> but instead obeys <a href="/wiki/Bose%E2%80%93Einstein_statistics" title="Bose&#8211;Einstein statistics">Bose&#8211;Einstein statistics</a>.\n</p><p>Photons are emitted in many natural processes. For example, when a charge is <a href="/wiki/Acceleration" title="Acceleration">accelerated</a> it emits <a href="/wiki/Synchrotron_radiation" title="Synchrotron radiation">synchrotron radiation</a>. During a <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecular</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atomic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">nuclear</a> transition to a lower <a href="/wiki/Energy_level" title="Energy level">energy level</a>, photons of various energy will be emitted, ranging from <a href="/wiki/Radio_wave" title="Radio wave">radio waves</a> to <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma rays</a>. Photons can also be emitted when a particle and its corresponding <a href="/wiki/Antiparticle" title="Antiparticle">antiparticle</a> are <a href="/wiki/Annihilation" title="Annihilation">annihilated</a> (for example, <a href="/wiki/Electron%E2%80%93positron_annihilation" title="Electron&#8211;positron annihilation">electron&#8211;positron annihilation</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Halliday_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Halliday-24">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>572,1114,1172</span></sup>\n</p><p>In empty space, the photon moves at <i>c</i> (the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a>) and its <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">momentum</a> are related by <span class="nowrap"><i>E</i> = <i>pc</i></span>, where <i>p</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Magnitude_(mathematics)" title="Magnitude (mathematics)">magnitude</a> of the momentum vector <b>p</b>. This derives from the following relativistic relation, with <span class="nowrap"><i>m</i> = 0</span>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[22]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle E^{2}=p^{2}c^{2}+m^{2}c^{4}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msup>\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<msup>\n<mi>p</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<msup>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>4</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mo>.</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle E^{2}=p^{2}c^{2}+m^{2}c^{4}.}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="E^{2}=p^{2} c^{2} + m^{2} c^{4}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7c87a47e0d2db63818810cb62589181abd473285" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:18.874ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>The energy and momentum of a photon depend only on its <a href="/wiki/Frequency" title="Frequency">frequency</a> (<i>&#957;</i>) or inversely, its <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelength</a> (<i>&#955;</i>):\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle E=\\hbar \\omega =h\\nu ={\\frac {hc}{\\lambda }}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi class="MJX-variant">&#8463;<!-- &#8463; --></mi>\n<mi>&#969;<!-- &#969; --></mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi>h</mi>\n<mi>&#957;<!-- &#957; --></mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mi>h</mi>\n<mi>c</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mi>&#955;<!-- &#955; --></mi>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle E=\\hbar \\omega =h\\nu ={\\frac {hc}{\\lambda }}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="E=\\hbar\\omega=h\\nu=\\frac{hc}{\\lambda}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ce3f4f21edb23ddd9a4a0941d0faf4a12cdaee74" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:19.576ex; height:5.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle {\\boldsymbol {p}}=\\hbar {\\boldsymbol {k}},}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="bold-italic">p</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi class="MJX-variant">&#8463;<!-- &#8463; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="bold-italic">k</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mo>,</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle {\\boldsymbol {p}}=\\hbar {\\boldsymbol {k}},}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\boldsymbol{p}=\\hbar\\boldsymbol{k}," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d8610388ca27ff6308f6965fbb7275d7f8b7754e" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; margin-left: -0.052ex; width:7.905ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>where <i><b>k</b></i> is the <a href="/wiki/Wave_vector" title="Wave vector">wave vector</a> (where the wave number <span class="nowrap"><i>k</i> = |<i><b>k</b></i>| = 2&#960;/<i>&#955;</i></span>), <span class="nowrap"><i>&#969;</i> = 2&#960;<i>&#957;</i></span> is the <a href="/wiki/Angular_frequency" title="Angular frequency">angular frequency</a>, and <span class="nowrap"><i>&#295;</i> = <i>h</i>/2&#960;</span> is the <a href="/wiki/Planck_constant" title="Planck constant">reduced Planck constant</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[23]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Since <i><b>p</b></i> points in the direction of the photon\'s propagation, the magnitude of the momentum is\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle p=\\hbar k={\\frac {h\\nu }{c}}={\\frac {h}{\\lambda }}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>p</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi class="MJX-variant">&#8463;<!-- &#8463; --></mi>\n<mi>k</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mi>h</mi>\n<mi>&#957;<!-- &#957; --></mi>\n</mrow>\n<mi>c</mi>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mi>h</mi>\n<mi>&#955;<!-- &#955; --></mi>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>.</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle p=\\hbar k={\\frac {h\\nu }{c}}={\\frac {h}{\\lambda }}.}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="p=\\hbar k=\\frac{h\\nu}{c}=\\frac{h}{\\lambda}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/798eb14a440c88545cb1a1493c2f62b1216c552b" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; margin-left: -0.089ex; width:19.317ex; height:5.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>The photon also carries a quantity called <a href="/wiki/Spin_angular_momentum_of_light" title="Spin angular momentum of light">spin angular momentum</a> that does not depend on its frequency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spin_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-spin-27">[24]</a></sup> The magnitude of its spin is <span class="nowrap">&#8730;<span style="border-top:1px solid; padding:0 0.1em;">2</span></span><i>&#295;</i> and the component measured along its direction of motion, its <a href="/wiki/Helicity_(particle_physics)" title="Helicity (particle physics)">helicity</a>, must be &#177;<i>&#295;</i>. These two possible helicities, called right-handed and left-handed, correspond to the two possible <a href="/wiki/Circular_polarization" title="Circular polarization">circular polarization</a> states of the photon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[25]</a></sup>\n</p><p>To illustrate the significance of these formulae, the annihilation of a particle with its antiparticle in free space must result in the creation of at least <i>two</i> photons for the following reason. In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Center_of_momentum_frame" title="Center of momentum frame">center of momentum frame</a>, the colliding antiparticles have no net momentum, whereas a single photon always has momentum (since, as we have seen, it is determined by the photon\'s frequency or wavelength, which cannot be zero). Hence, <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">conservation of momentum</a> (or equivalently, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Translational_invariance" title="Translational invariance">translational invariance</a>) requires that at least two photons are created, with zero net momentum. (However, it is possible if the system interacts with another particle or field for the annihilation to produce one photon, as when a positron annihilates with a bound atomic electron, it is possible for only one photon to be emitted, as the nuclear Coulomb field breaks translational symmetry.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Griffiths2008_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Griffiths2008-29">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>64&#8211;65</span></sup> The energy of the two photons, or, equivalently, their frequency, may be determined from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservation_law_(physics)" title="Conservation law (physics)">conservation of four-momentum</a>. Seen another way, the photon can be considered as its own antiparticle. The reverse process, <a href="/wiki/Pair_production" title="Pair production">pair production</a>, is the dominant mechanism by which high-energy photons such as <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma rays</a> lose energy while passing through matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[27]</a></sup> That process is the reverse of "annihilation to one photon" allowed in the electric field of an atomic nucleus.\n</p><p>The classical formulae for the energy and momentum of <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic radiation</a> can be re-expressed in terms of photon events. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Radiation_pressure" title="Radiation pressure">pressure of electromagnetic radiation</a> on an object derives from the transfer of photon momentum per unit time and unit area to that object, since pressure is force per unit area and force is the change in <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">momentum</a> per unit time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[28]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Each photon carries two distinct and independent forms of <a href="/wiki/Angular_momentum_of_light" title="Angular momentum of light">angular momentum of light</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Spin_angular_momentum_of_light" title="Spin angular momentum of light">spin angular momentum of light</a> of a particular photon is always either +<i>&#295;</i> or &#8722;<i>&#295;</i>.\nThe <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light_orbital_angular_momentum" title="Light orbital angular momentum">light orbital angular momentum</a> of a particular photon can be any integer <i>N</i>, including zero.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[29]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Experimental_checks_on_photon_mass">Experimental checks on photon mass</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Experimental checks on photon mass">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Current commonly accepted physical theories imply or assume the photon to be strictly massless. If the photon is not a strictly massless particle, it would not move at the exact speed of light, <i>c</i>, in vacuum. Its speed would be lower and depend on its frequency. Relativity would be unaffected by this; the so-called speed of light, <i>c</i>, would then not be the actual speed at which light moves, but a constant of nature which is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upper_bound" title="Upper bound">upper bound</a> on speed that any object could theoretically attain in spacetime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[30]</a></sup> Thus, it would still be the speed of spacetime ripples (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_waves" title="Gravitational waves">gravitational waves</a> and <a href="/wiki/Graviton" title="Graviton">gravitons</a>), but it would not be the speed of photons.\n</p><p>If a photon did have non-zero mass, there would be other effects as well. <a href="/wiki/Coulomb%27s_law" title="Coulomb\'s law">Coulomb\'s law</a> would be modified and the <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_field" title="Electromagnetic field">electromagnetic field</a> would have an extra physical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Degree_of_freedom" title="Degree of freedom">degree of freedom</a>. These effects yield more sensitive experimental probes of the photon mass than the frequency dependence of the speed of light. If Coulomb\'s law is not exactly valid, then that would allow the presence of an <a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">electric field</a> to exist within a hollow conductor when it is subjected to an external electric field. This thus allows one to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tests_of_electromagnetism" title="Tests of electromagnetism">test</a> Coulomb\'s law to very high precision.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[31]</a></sup> A null result of such an experiment has set a limit of <span class="nowrap"><i>m</i> &#8818; <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6986100000000000000&#9824;</span>10<sup>&#8722;14</sup>&#160;eV/<i>c</i><sup>2</sup></span></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[32]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Sharper upper limits on the speed of light have been obtained in experiments designed to detect effects caused by the galactic <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_potential" title="Magnetic potential">vector potential</a>. Although the galactic vector potential is very large because the galactic <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a> exists on very great length scales, only the magnetic field would be observable if the photon is massless. In the case that the photon has mass, the mass term <span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;">1</span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;">2</span></span><i>m</i><sup>2</sup><i>A</i><sub><i>&#956;</i></sub><i>A</i><sup><i>&#956;</i></sup> would affect the galactic plasma. The fact that no such effects are seen implies an upper bound on the photon mass of <span class="nowrap"><i>m</i> &lt; <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6973300000000000000&#9824;</span>3<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>&#8722;27</sup>&#160;eV/<i>c</i><sup>2</sup></span></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[33]</a></sup> The galactic vector potential can also be probed directly by measuring the torque exerted on a magnetized ring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[34]</a></sup> Such methods were used to obtain the sharper upper limit of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6982100000000000000&#9824;</span>10<sup>&#8722;18</sup>&#160;eV/<i>c</i><sup>2</sup></span> (the equivalent of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6973107000000000000&#9824;</span>1.07<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>&#8722;27</sup>&#160;atomic mass units</span>) given by the <a href="/wiki/Particle_Data_Group" title="Particle Data Group">Particle Data Group</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amsler_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-amsler-38">[35]</a></sup>\n</p><p>These sharp limits from the non-observation of the effects caused by the galactic vector potential have been shown to be model-dependent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[36]</a></sup> If the photon mass is generated via the <a href="/wiki/Higgs_mechanism" title="Higgs mechanism">Higgs mechanism</a> then the upper limit of <span class="nowrap"><i>m</i> &#8818; <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6986100000000000000&#9824;</span>10<sup>&#8722;14</sup>&#160;eV/<i>c</i><sup>2</sup></span></span> from the test of Coulomb\'s law is valid.\n</p><p>Photons inside <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superconductors" title="Superconductors">superconductors</a> develop a nonzero <a href="/wiki/Effective_mass_(solid-state_physics)" title="Effective mass (solid-state physics)">effective rest mass</a>; as a result, electromagnetic forces become short-range inside superconductors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[37]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supernova/Acceleration_Probe" title="Supernova/Acceleration Probe">Supernova/Acceleration Probe</a></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_development">Historical development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Historical development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">Light</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Young_Diffraction.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="882" data-file-width="1988" decoding="async" height="89" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Young_Diffraction.png/200px-Young_Diffraction.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Young_Diffraction.png/300px-Young_Diffraction.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Young_Diffraction.png/400px-Young_Diffraction.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Young_Diffraction.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Young_(scientist)" title="Thomas Young (scientist)">Thomas Young</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Double-slit_experiment" title="Double-slit experiment">double-slit experiment</a> in 1801 showed that light can act as a <a href="/wiki/Wave" title="Wave">wave</a>, helping to invalidate early <a href="/wiki/Elementary_particle" title="Elementary particle">particle</a> theories of light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Halliday_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-Halliday-24">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>964</span></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>In most theories up to the eighteenth century, light was pictured as being made up of particles. Since <a href="/wiki/Subatomic_particle" title="Subatomic particle">particle</a> models cannot easily account for the <a href="/wiki/Refraction" title="Refraction">refraction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diffraction" title="Diffraction">diffraction</a> and <a href="/wiki/Birefringence" title="Birefringence">birefringence</a> of light, wave theories of light were proposed by <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="Ren&#233; Descartes">Ren&#233; Descartes</a> (1637),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[38]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hooke" title="Robert Hooke">Robert Hooke</a> (1665),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[39]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Christiaan_Huygens" title="Christiaan Huygens">Christiaan Huygens</a> (1678);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[40]</a></sup> however, particle models remained dominant, chiefly due to the influence of <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Newton1730_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Newton1730-44">[41]</a></sup> In the early nineteenth century, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Young_(scientist)" title="Thomas Young (scientist)">Thomas Young</a> and <a href="/wiki/Augustin-Jean_Fresnel" title="Augustin-Jean Fresnel">August Fresnel</a> clearly demonstrated the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interference_(wave_propagation)" title="Interference (wave propagation)">interference</a> and diffraction of light and by 1850 wave models were generally accepted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[42]</a></sup> In 1865, <a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">James Clerk Maxwell</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Maxwell%27s_equations" title="Maxwell\'s equations">prediction</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-maxwell_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-maxwell-46">[43]</a></sup> that light was an electromagnetic wave&#8212;which was confirmed experimentally in 1888 by <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Hertz" title="Heinrich Hertz">Heinrich Hertz</a>\'s detection of <a href="/wiki/Radio" title="Radio">radio waves</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hertz_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-hertz-47">[44]</a></sup>&#8212;seemed to be the final blow to particle models of light.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:342px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Light-wave.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="290" data-file-width="554" decoding="async" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Light-wave.svg/340px-Light-wave.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Light-wave.svg/510px-Light-wave.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Light-wave.svg/680px-Light-wave.svg.png 2x" width="340"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Light-wave.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In 1900, <a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">Maxwell\'s</a> <a href="/wiki/Maxwell%27s_equations" title="Maxwell\'s equations">theoretical model of light</a> as oscillating <a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">electric</a> and <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic fields</a> seemed complete. However, several observations could not be explained by any wave model of <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic radiation</a>, leading to the idea that light-energy was packaged into <i>quanta</i> described by E=h&#957;. Later experiments showed that these light-quanta also carry momentum and, thus, can be considered <a href="/wiki/Elementary_particle" title="Elementary particle">particles</a>: the <i>photon</i> concept was born, leading to a deeper understanding of the electric and magnetic fields themselves.</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_wave_equation" title="Electromagnetic wave equation">Maxwell wave theory</a>, however, does not account for <i>all</i> properties of light. The Maxwell theory predicts that the energy of a light wave depends only on its <a href="/wiki/Intensity_(physics)" title="Intensity (physics)">intensity</a>, not on its <a href="/wiki/Frequency" title="Frequency">frequency</a>; nevertheless, several independent types of experiments show that the energy imparted by light to atoms depends only on the light\'s frequency, not on its intensity. For example, <a href="/wiki/Photochemistry" title="Photochemistry">some chemical reactions</a> are provoked only by light of frequency higher than a certain threshold; light of frequency lower than the threshold, no matter how intense, does not initiate the reaction. Similarly, electrons can be ejected from a metal plate by shining light of sufficiently high frequency on it (the <a href="/wiki/Photoelectric_effect" title="Photoelectric effect">photoelectric effect</a>); the energy of the ejected electron is related only to the light\'s frequency, not to its intensity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[d]</a></sup>\n</p><p>At the same time, investigations of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blackbody_radiation" title="Blackbody radiation">blackbody radiation</a> carried out over four decades (1860&#8211;1900) by various researchers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wien1911_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wien1911-50">[46]</a></sup> culminated in <a href="/wiki/Max_Planck" title="Max Planck">Max Planck</a>\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planck%27s_constant" title="Planck\'s constant">hypothesis</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Planck1901_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Planck1901-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Planck1918_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Planck1918-51">[47]</a></sup> that the energy of <i>any</i> system that absorbs or emits electromagnetic radiation of frequency <i>&#957;</i> is an integer multiple of an energy quantum <span class="nowrap"><i>E</i> = <i>h&#957;</i></span>. As shown by <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Einstein1905_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Einstein1905-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Einstein1909_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Einstein1909-52">[48]</a></sup> some form of energy quantization <i>must</i> be assumed to account for the thermal equilibrium observed between matter and <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic radiation</a>; for this explanation of the <a href="/wiki/Photoelectric_effect" title="Photoelectric effect">photoelectric effect</a>, Einstein received the 1921 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize" title="Nobel Prize">Nobel Prize</a> in physics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[49]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Since the Maxwell theory of light allows for all possible energies of electromagnetic radiation, most physicists assumed initially that the energy quantization resulted from some unknown constraint on the matter that absorbs or emits the radiation. In 1905, Einstein was the first to propose that energy quantization was a property of electromagnetic radiation itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Einstein1905_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-Einstein1905-9">[9]</a></sup> Although he accepted the validity of Maxwell\'s theory, Einstein pointed out that many anomalous experiments could be explained if the <i>energy</i> of a Maxwellian light wave were localized into point-like quanta that move independently of one another, even if the wave itself is spread continuously over space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Einstein1905_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-Einstein1905-9">[9]</a></sup> In 1909<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Einstein1909_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-Einstein1909-52">[48]</a></sup> and 1916,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Einstein1916b_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Einstein1916b-54">[50]</a></sup> Einstein showed that, if <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planck%27s_law_of_black-body_radiation" title="Planck\'s law of black-body radiation">Planck\'s law of black-body radiation</a> is accepted, the energy quanta must also carry <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">momentum</a> <span class="nowrap"><i>p</i> = <i>h</i>/<i>&#955;</i></span>, making them full-fledged <a href="/wiki/Elementary_particle" title="Elementary particle">particles</a>. This photon momentum was observed experimentally<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Compton1923_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Compton1923-55">[51]</a></sup> by <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Compton" title="Arthur Compton">Arthur Compton</a>, for which he received the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize" title="Nobel Prize">Nobel Prize</a> in 1927. The pivotal question was then: how to unify Maxwell\'s wave theory of light with its experimentally observed particle nature? The answer to this question occupied <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> for the rest of his life,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pais1982_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pais1982-56">[52]</a></sup> and was solved in <a href="/wiki/Quantum_electrodynamics" title="Quantum electrodynamics">quantum electrodynamics</a> and its successor, the <a href="/wiki/Standard_Model" title="Standard Model">Standard Model</a> (see <i><a href="#Second_quantization">&#167;&#160;Second quantization</a></i> and <i><a href="#The_photon_as_a_gauge_boson">&#167;&#160;The photon as a gauge boson</a></i>, below).\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Einstein.27s_light_quantum"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Einstein\'s_light_quantum">Einstein\'s light quantum</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Einstein\'s light quantum">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Unlike Planck, Einstein entertained the possibility that there might be actual physical quanta of light&#8212;what we now call photons. He noticed that a light quantum with energy proportional to its frequency would explain a number of troubling puzzles and paradoxes, including an unpublished law by Stokes, the <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet_catastrophe" title="Ultraviolet catastrophe">ultraviolet catastrophe</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Photoelectric_effect" title="Photoelectric effect">photoelectric effect</a>. Stokes\'s law said simply that the frequency of fluorescent light cannot be greater than the frequency of the light (usually ultraviolet) inducing it. Einstein eliminated the ultraviolet catastrophe by imagining a gas of photons behaving like a gas of electrons that he had previously considered. He was advised by a colleague to be careful how he wrote up this paper, in order to not challenge Planck, a powerful figure in physics, too directly, and indeed the warning was justified, as Planck never forgave him for writing it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[53]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_objections">Early objections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Early objections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bohr_atom_model.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="270" data-file-width="310" decoding="async" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Bohr_atom_model.svg/250px-Bohr_atom_model.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Bohr_atom_model.svg/375px-Bohr_atom_model.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Bohr_atom_model.svg/500px-Bohr_atom_model.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bohr_atom_model.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Up to 1923, most physicists were reluctant to accept that light itself was quantized. Instead, they tried to explain photon behavior by quantizing only <i>matter</i>, as in the <a href="/wiki/Bohr_model" title="Bohr model">Bohr model</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_atom" title="Hydrogen atom">hydrogen atom</a> (shown here). Even though these semiclassical models were only a first approximation, they were accurate for simple systems and they led to <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Einstein\'s 1905 predictions were verified experimentally in several ways in the first two decades of the 20th century, as recounted in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robert_Millikan" title="Robert Millikan">Robert Millikan</a>\'s Nobel lecture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Millikan1923_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Millikan1923-58">[54]</a></sup> However, before <a href="/wiki/Compton_scattering" title="Compton scattering">Compton\'s experiment</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Compton1923_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-Compton1923-55">[51]</a></sup> showed that photons carried <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">momentum</a> proportional to their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wave_number" title="Wave number">wave number</a> (1922), most physicists were reluctant to believe that <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic radiation</a> itself might be particulate. (See, for example, the Nobel lectures of <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Wien" title="Wilhelm Wien">Wien</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wien1911_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wien1911-50">[46]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Max_Planck" title="Max Planck">Planck</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Planck1918_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Planck1918-51">[47]</a></sup> and Millikan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Millikan1923_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Millikan1923-58">[54]</a></sup>) Instead, there was a widespread belief that energy quantization resulted from some unknown constraint on the matter that absorbed or emitted radiation. Attitudes changed over time. In part, the change can be traced to experiments such as <a href="/wiki/Compton_scattering" title="Compton scattering">Compton scattering</a>, where it was much more difficult not to ascribe quantization to light itself to explain the observed results.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[55]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Even after Compton\'s experiment, <a href="/wiki/Niels_Bohr" title="Niels Bohr">Niels Bohr</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hendrik_Anthony_Kramers" title="Hendrik Anthony Kramers">Hendrik Kramers</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_C._Slater" title="John C. Slater">John Slater</a> made one last attempt to preserve the Maxwellian continuous electromagnetic field model of light, the so-called <a href="/wiki/BKS_theory" title="BKS theory">BKS model</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bohr1924_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bohr1924-60">[56]</a></sup> To account for the data then available, two drastic hypotheses had to be made:\n</p>\n<ol><li><b>Energy and momentum are conserved only on the average in interactions between matter and radiation, but not in elementary processes such as absorption and emission.</b> This allows one to reconcile the discontinuously changing energy of the atom (the jump between energy states) with the continuous release of energy as radiation.</li>\n<li><b>Causality is abandoned</b>. For example, <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_emission" title="Spontaneous emission">spontaneous emissions</a> are merely <a href="/wiki/Stimulated_emission" title="Stimulated emission">emissions stimulated</a> by a "virtual" electromagnetic field.</li></ol>\n<p>However, refined Compton experiments showed that energy&#8211;momentum is conserved extraordinarily well in elementary processes; and also that the jolting of the electron and the generation of a new photon in <a href="/wiki/Compton_scattering" title="Compton scattering">Compton scattering</a> obey causality to within 10 <a href="/wiki/Picosecond" title="Picosecond">ps</a>. Accordingly, Bohr and his co-workers gave their model "as honorable a funeral as possible".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pais1982_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pais1982-56">[52]</a></sup> Nevertheless, the failures of the BKS model inspired <a href="/wiki/Werner_Heisenberg" title="Werner Heisenberg">Werner Heisenberg</a> in his development of <a href="/wiki/Matrix_mechanics" title="Matrix mechanics">matrix mechanics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heisenberg1932_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heisenberg1932-61">[57]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A few physicists persisted<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mandel1976_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mandel1976-62">[58]</a></sup> in developing semiclassical models in which <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic radiation</a> is not quantized, but matter appears to obey the laws of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a>. Although the evidence from chemical and physical experiments for the existence of photons was overwhelming by the 1970s, this evidence could not be considered as <i>absolutely</i> definitive; since it relied on the interaction of light with matter, and a sufficiently complete theory of matter could in principle account for the evidence. Nevertheless, <i>all</i> semiclassical theories were refuted definitively in the 1970s and 1980s by photon-correlation experiments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[e]</a></sup> Hence, Einstein\'s hypothesis that quantization is a property of light itself is considered to be proven.\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Wave.E2.80.93particle_duality_and_uncertainty_principles"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Wave&#8211;particle_duality_and_uncertainty_principles">Wave&#8211;particle duality and uncertainty principles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Wave&#8211;particle duality and uncertainty principles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Wave%E2%80%93particle_duality" title="Wave&#8211;particle duality">Wave&#8211;particle duality</a>, <a href="/wiki/Squeezed_coherent_state" title="Squeezed coherent state">Squeezed coherent state</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uncertainty_principle" title="Uncertainty principle">Uncertainty principle</a>, and <a href="/wiki/De_Broglie%E2%80%93Bohm_theory" title="De Broglie&#8211;Bohm theory">De Broglie&#8211;Bohm theory</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mach-Zehnder_photons_animation.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="220" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a0/Mach-Zehnder_photons_animation.gif" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mach-Zehnder_photons_animation.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Photons in a <a href="/wiki/Mach%E2%80%93Zehnder_interferometer" title="Mach&#8211;Zehnder interferometer">Mach&#8211;Zehnder interferometer</a> exhibit wave-like interference and particle-like detection at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photon-counting_detector" title="Photon-counting detector">single-photon detectors</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Photons, like all quantum objects, exhibit wave-like and particle-like properties. Their dual wave&#8211;particle nature can be difficult to visualize. The photon displays clearly wave-like phenomena such as <a href="/wiki/Diffraction" title="Diffraction">diffraction</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interference_(wave_propagation)" title="Interference (wave propagation)">interference</a> on the length scale of its wavelength. For example, a single photon passing through a <a href="/wiki/Double-slit_experiment" title="Double-slit experiment">double-slit experiment</a> exhibits interference phenomena but only if no measure was made at the slit. A single photon passing through a double-slit experiment lands on the screen with a <a href="/wiki/Probability_distribution" title="Probability distribution">probability distribution</a> given by its interference pattern determined by <a href="/wiki/Maxwell%27s_equations" title="Maxwell\'s equations">Maxwell\'s equations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor1909_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor1909-64">[59]</a></sup> However, experiments confirm that the photon is <i>not</i> a short pulse of electromagnetic radiation; it does not spread out as it propagates, nor does it divide when it encounters a <a href="/wiki/Beam_splitter" title="Beam splitter">beam splitter</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saleh_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Saleh-65">[60]</a></sup> Rather, the photon seems to be a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Point-like_particle" title="Point-like particle">point-like particle</a> since it is absorbed or emitted <i>as a whole</i> by arbitrarily small systems, systems much smaller than its wavelength, such as an atomic nucleus (&#8776;10<sup>&#8722;15</sup> m across) or even the point-like <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electron</a>. Nevertheless, the photon is <i>not</i> a point-like particle whose trajectory is shaped probabilistically by the <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_field" title="Electromagnetic field">electromagnetic field</a>, as conceived by <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Einstein</a> and others; that hypothesis was also refuted by the photon-correlation experiments cited above. According to our present understanding, the electromagnetic field itself is produced by photons, which in turn result from a local <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gauge_symmetry" title="Gauge symmetry">gauge symmetry</a> and the laws of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_field_theory" title="Quantum field theory">quantum field theory</a> (see <i><a href="#Second_quantization">&#167;&#160;Second quantization</a></i> and <i><a href="#The_photon_as_a_gauge_boson">&#167;&#160;The photon as a gauge boson</a></i> below).\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gamma-ray-microscope.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="320" data-file-width="240" decoding="async" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Gamma-ray-microscope.svg/200px-Gamma-ray-microscope.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Gamma-ray-microscope.svg/300px-Gamma-ray-microscope.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Gamma-ray-microscope.svg/400px-Gamma-ray-microscope.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gamma-ray-microscope.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Werner_Heisenberg" title="Werner Heisenberg">Heisenberg\'s</a> <a href="/wiki/Thought_experiment" title="Thought experiment">thought experiment</a> for locating an <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electron</a> (shown in blue) with a high-resolution gamma-ray microscope. The incoming <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma ray</a> (shown in green) is scattered by the electron up into the microscope\'s <a href="/wiki/Angular_aperture" title="Angular aperture">aperture angle</a> &#952;. The scattered gamma ray is shown in red. <a href="/wiki/Optics" title="Optics">Classical optics</a> shows that the electron position can be resolved only up to an uncertainty &#916;x that depends on &#952; and the <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelength</a> &#955; of the incoming light.</div></div></div>\n<p>A key element of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a> is <a href="/wiki/Werner_Heisenberg" title="Werner Heisenberg">Heisenberg\'s</a> <a href="/wiki/Uncertainty_principle" title="Uncertainty principle">uncertainty principle</a>, which forbids the simultaneous measurement of the position and momentum of a particle along the same direction. Remarkably, the uncertainty principle for charged, material particles <i>requires</i> the quantization of light into photons, and even the frequency dependence of the photon\'s energy and momentum.\n</p><p><span id="Locating_an_electron_with_an_ideal_microscope"></span>An elegant illustration of the uncertainty principle is Heisenberg\'s <a href="/wiki/Thought_experiment" title="Thought experiment">thought experiment</a> for locating an electron with an ideal microscope.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heisenberg1927_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heisenberg1927-66">[61]</a></sup> The position of the electron can be determined to within the <a href="/wiki/Angular_resolution" title="Angular resolution">resolving power</a> of the microscope, which is given by a formula from classical <a href="/wiki/Optics" title="Optics">optics</a>\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\Delta x\\sim {\\frac {\\lambda }{\\sin \\theta }}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#916;<!-- &#916; --></mi>\n<mi>x</mi>\n<mo>&#8764;<!-- &#8764; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mi>&#955;<!-- &#955; --></mi>\n<mrow>\n<mi>sin</mi>\n<mo>&#8289;<!-- &#8289; --></mo>\n<mi>&#952;<!-- &#952; --></mi>\n</mrow>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\Delta x\\sim {\\frac {\\lambda }{\\sin \\theta }}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\n\\Delta x \\sim \\frac{\\lambda}{\\sin \\theta}\n" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e272651ec82f0cb6e7b113b077b2551112ceb534" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:11.533ex; height:5.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>where &#952; is the <a href="/wiki/Angular_aperture" title="Angular aperture">aperture angle</a> of the microscope and &#955; is the wavelength of the light used to observe the electron. Thus, the position uncertainty <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\Delta x}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#916;<!-- &#916; --></mi>\n<mi>x</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\Delta x}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\Delta x" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f3890eb866b6258d7a304fc34c70ee3fb3a81a70" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:3.266ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> can be made arbitrarily small by reducing the wavelength &#955;. Even if the momentum of the electron is initially known, the light impinging on the electron will give it a momentum "kick" <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\Delta p}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#916;<!-- &#916; --></mi>\n<mi>p</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\Delta p}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\Delta p" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0758c326125ad3d8b96e515c7fd69164ec587b81" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:3.105ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> of some unknown amount, rendering the momentum of the electron uncertain. If light were <i>not</i> quantized into photons, the uncertainty <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\Delta p}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#916;<!-- &#916; --></mi>\n<mi>p</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\Delta p}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\Delta p" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0758c326125ad3d8b96e515c7fd69164ec587b81" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:3.105ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> could be made arbitrarily small by reducing the light\'s intensity. In that case, since the wavelength and intensity of light can be varied independently, one could simultaneously determine the position and momentum to arbitrarily high accuracy, violating the <a href="/wiki/Uncertainty_principle" title="Uncertainty principle">uncertainty principle</a>. By contrast, Einstein\'s formula for photon momentum preserves the uncertainty principle; since the photon is scattered anywhere within the aperture, the uncertainty of momentum transferred equals\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\Delta p\\sim p_{\\text{photon}}\\sin \\theta ={\\frac {h}{\\lambda }}\\sin \\theta }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#916;<!-- &#916; --></mi>\n<mi>p</mi>\n<mo>&#8764;<!-- &#8764; --></mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>p</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mtext>photon</mtext>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mi>sin</mi>\n<mo>&#8289;<!-- &#8289; --></mo>\n<mi>&#952;<!-- &#952; --></mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mi>h</mi>\n<mi>&#955;<!-- &#955; --></mi>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mi>sin</mi>\n<mo>&#8289;<!-- &#8289; --></mo>\n<mi>&#952;<!-- &#952; --></mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\Delta p\\sim p_{\\text{photon}}\\sin \\theta ={\\frac {h}{\\lambda }}\\sin \\theta }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\n\\Delta p \\sim p_{\\text{photon}} \\sin\\theta=\\frac{h}{\\lambda} \\sin\\theta\n" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a5c51a95021924d06a66c7539bae76aa27a2d387" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:27.361ex; height:5.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>giving the product <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\Delta x\\Delta p\\,\\sim \\,h}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#916;<!-- &#916; --></mi>\n<mi>x</mi>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#916;<!-- &#916; --></mi>\n<mi>p</mi>\n<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>\n<mo>&#8764;<!-- &#8764; --></mo>\n<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>\n<mi>h</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\Delta x\\Delta p\\,\\sim \\,h}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\Delta x \\Delta p \\, \\sim \\, h" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ec4f4061c3f79e6e3f3bad78e5ea31dd790758b6" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:11.582ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>, which is Heisenberg\'s uncertainty principle. Thus, the entire world is quantized; both matter and fields must obey a consistent set of quantum laws, if either one is to be quantized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[62]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The analogous uncertainty principle for photons forbids the simultaneous measurement of the number <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle n}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>n</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle n}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="n" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.395ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> of photons (see <a href="/wiki/Fock_state" title="Fock state">Fock state</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Photon#Second_quantization" title="Photon">Second quantization</a> section below) in an electromagnetic wave and the phase <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\phi }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>&#981;<!-- &#981; --></mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\phi }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\phi " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/72b1f30316670aee6270a28334bdf4f5072cdde4" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:1.385ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> of that wave\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\Delta n\\Delta \\phi &gt;1}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#916;<!-- &#916; --></mi>\n<mi>n</mi>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#916;<!-- &#916; --></mi>\n<mi>&#981;<!-- &#981; --></mi>\n<mo>&gt;</mo>\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\Delta n\\Delta \\phi &gt;1}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\n\\Delta n \\Delta \\phi &gt; 1\n" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/92e11d8da5d69f65091e57fa96bf65b872027323" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:10.913ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>See <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coherent_state" title="Coherent state">coherent state</a> and <a href="/wiki/Squeezed_coherent_state" title="Squeezed coherent state">squeezed coherent state</a> for more details.\n</p><p>Both photons and electrons create analogous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interference_(wave_propagation)" title="Interference (wave propagation)">interference patterns</a> when passed through a <a href="/wiki/Double-slit_experiment" title="Double-slit experiment">double-slit experiment</a>. For photons, this corresponds to the interference of a <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_wave_equation" title="Electromagnetic wave equation">Maxwell light wave</a> whereas, for material particles (electron), this corresponds to the interference of the <a href="/wiki/Schr%C3%B6dinger_equation" title="Schr&#246;dinger equation">Schr&#246;dinger wave equation</a>. Although this similarity might suggest that <a href="/wiki/Maxwell%27s_equations" title="Maxwell\'s equations">Maxwell\'s equations</a> describing the photon\'s electromagnetic wave are simply Schr&#246;dinger\'s equation for photons, most physicists do not agree.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[64]</a></sup> For one thing, they are mathematically different; most obviously, Schr&#246;dinger\'s one equation for the electron solves for a <a href="/wiki/Complex_number" title="Complex number">complex</a> <a href="/wiki/Field_(physics)" title="Field (physics)">field</a>, whereas Maxwell\'s four equations solve for <a href="/wiki/Real_number" title="Real number">real</a> fields. More generally, the normal concept of a Schr&#246;dinger <a href="/wiki/Probability_amplitude" title="Probability amplitude">probability</a> <a href="/wiki/Wave_function" title="Wave function">wave function</a> cannot be applied to photons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[65]</a></sup> As photons are massless, they cannot be localized without being destroyed; technically, photons cannot have a position eigenstate <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle |\\mathbf {r} \\rangle }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo stretchy="false">|</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="bold">r</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">&#10217;<!-- &#10217; --></mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle |\\mathbf {r} \\rangle }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="|\\mathbf{r} \\rangle" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3d86ef4665f4fd222f128b598d66776b211e9dac" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:2.654ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span>, and, thus, the normal Heisenberg uncertainty principle <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\Delta x\\Delta p&gt;h/2}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#916;<!-- &#916; --></mi>\n<mi>x</mi>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#916;<!-- &#916; --></mi>\n<mi>p</mi>\n<mo>&gt;</mo>\n<mi>h</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\Delta x\\Delta p&gt;h/2}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\Delta x \\Delta p &gt; h/2" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a6d7e104c9329b417bb6e84d36e2bfe21ba2aeff" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:13.133ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> does not pertain to photons.
