A few substitute wave functions have been suggested for the photon,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[69]</a></sup> but they have not come into general use. Instead, physicists generally accept the second-quantized theory of photons described below, <a href="/wiki/Quantum_electrodynamics" title="Quantum electrodynamics">quantum electrodynamics</a>, in which photons are quantized excitations of electromagnetic modes.\n</p><p>Another interpretation, that avoids duality, is the <a href="/wiki/De_Broglie%E2%80%93Bohm_theory" title="De Broglie&#8211;Bohm theory">De Broglie&#8211;Bohm theory</a>: known also as the <i>pilot-wave model</i>. In that theory, the photon is both, wave and particle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[70]</a></sup> <i>"This idea seems to me so natural and simple, to resolve the wave-particle dilemma in such a clear and ordinary way, that it is a great mystery to me that it was so generally ignored"</i>, J.S. Bell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bell87_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-bell87-76">[71]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Bose.E2.80.93Einstein_model_of_a_photon_gas"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Bose&#8211;Einstein_model_of_a_photon_gas">Bose&#8211;Einstein model of a photon gas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Bose&#8211;Einstein model of a photon gas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Bose_gas" title="Bose gas">Bose gas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bose%E2%80%93Einstein_statistics" title="Bose&#8211;Einstein statistics">Bose&#8211;Einstein statistics</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spin-statistics_theorem" title="Spin-statistics theorem">Spin-statistics theorem</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gas_in_a_box" title="Gas in a box">Gas in a box</a></div>\n<p>In 1924, <a href="/wiki/Satyendra_Nath_Bose" title="Satyendra Nath Bose">Satyendra Nath Bose</a> derived <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planck%27s_law_of_black-body_radiation" title="Planck\'s law of black-body radiation">Planck\'s law of black-body radiation</a> without using any electromagnetism, but rather by using a modification of coarse-grained counting of <a href="/wiki/Phase_space" title="Phase space">phase space</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bose1924_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bose1924-77">[72]</a></sup> Einstein showed that this modification is equivalent to assuming that photons are rigorously identical and that it implied a "mysterious non-local interaction",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Einstein1924_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Einstein1924-78">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Einstein1925_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Einstein1925-79">[74]</a></sup> now understood as the requirement for a <a href="/wiki/Identical_particles" title="Identical particles">symmetric quantum mechanical state</a>. This work led to the concept of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coherent_state" title="Coherent state">coherent states</a> and the development of the laser. In the same papers, Einstein extended Bose\'s formalism to material particles (<a href="/wiki/Boson" title="Boson">bosons</a>) and predicted that they would condense into their lowest <a href="/wiki/Quantum_state" title="Quantum state">quantum state</a> at low enough temperatures; this <a href="/wiki/Bose%E2%80%93Einstein_condensate" title="Bose&#8211;Einstein condensate">Bose&#8211;Einstein condensation</a> was observed experimentally in 1995.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[75]</a></sup> It was later used by <a href="/wiki/Lene_Hau" title="Lene Hau">Lene Hau</a> to slow, and then completely stop, light in 1999<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[76]</a></sup> and 2001.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[77]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The modern view on this is that photons are, by virtue of their integer spin, <a href="/wiki/Boson" title="Boson">bosons</a> (as opposed to <a href="/wiki/Fermion" title="Fermion">fermions</a> with half-integer spin). By the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spin-statistics_theorem" title="Spin-statistics theorem">spin-statistics theorem</a>, all bosons obey Bose&#8211;Einstein statistics (whereas all fermions obey <a href="/wiki/Fermi%E2%80%93Dirac_statistics" title="Fermi&#8211;Dirac statistics">Fermi&#8211;Dirac statistics</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[78]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Stimulated_and_spontaneous_emission">Stimulated and spontaneous emission</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Stimulated and spontaneous emission">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Stimulated_emission" title="Stimulated emission">Stimulated emission</a> and <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">Laser</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stimulatedemission.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="281" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Stimulatedemission.png/400px-Stimulatedemission.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Stimulatedemission.png/600px-Stimulatedemission.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8a/Stimulatedemission.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stimulatedemission.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Stimulated_emission" title="Stimulated emission">Stimulated emission</a> (in which photons "clone" themselves) was predicted by Einstein in his kinetic analysis, and led to the development of the <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">laser</a>. Einstein\'s derivation inspired further developments in the quantum treatment of light, which led to the statistical interpretation of quantum mechanics.</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1916, Albert Einstein showed that Planck\'s radiation law could be derived from a semi-classical, statistical treatment of photons and atoms, which implies a link between the rates at which atoms emit and absorb photons. The condition follows from the assumption that functions of the emission and absorption of radiation by the atoms are independent of each other, and that thermal equilibrium is made by way of the radiation\'s interaction with the atoms. Consider a cavity in <a href="/wiki/Thermal_equilibrium" title="Thermal equilibrium">thermal equilibrium</a> with all parts of itself and filled with <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic radiation</a> and that the atoms can emit and absorb that radiation. Thermal equilibrium requires that the energy density <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\rho (\\nu )}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>&#961;<!-- &#961; --></mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>&#957;<!-- &#957; --></mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\rho (\\nu )}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\rho(\\nu)" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/771818acdb33bf67ca39d6f197a871dae92752b3" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:4.243ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> of photons with frequency <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\nu }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>&#957;<!-- &#957; --></mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\nu }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\nu " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c15bbbb971240cf328aba572178f091684585468" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.232ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> (which is proportional to their <a href="/wiki/Number_density" title="Number density">number density</a>) is, on average, constant in time; hence, the rate at which photons of any particular frequency are <i>emitted</i> must equal the rate at which they <i>absorb</i> them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Einstein1916a_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Einstein1916a-84">[79]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Einstein began by postulating simple proportionality relations for the different reaction rates involved. In his model, the rate <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle R_{ji}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>R</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>j</mi>\n<mi>i</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle R_{ji}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="R_{ji}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/07aa016887d6fd7c4d7f5041899cb34d8ba1154e" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:3.241ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> for a system to <i>absorb</i> a photon of frequency <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\nu }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>&#957;<!-- &#957; --></mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\nu }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\nu " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c15bbbb971240cf328aba572178f091684585468" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.232ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> and transition from a lower energy <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle E_{j}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>j</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle E_{j}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="E_{j}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a01babf2293d37d9ff73b61202cfc3a3933eaf19" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:2.625ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> to a higher energy <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle E_{i}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle E_{i}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="E_{i}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8ba9f6e3041b052cf13a0ede4ecf35fb4c9cd16c" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.515ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> is proportional to the number <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle N_{j}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>N</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>j</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle N_{j}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="N_{j}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e42efe207e17a74b4c17b1aa0cced7a84501bec9" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:2.776ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> of atoms with energy <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle E_{j}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>j</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle E_{j}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="E_{j}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a01babf2293d37d9ff73b61202cfc3a3933eaf19" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:2.625ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> and to the energy density <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\rho (\\nu )}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>&#961;<!-- &#961; --></mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>&#957;<!-- &#957; --></mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\rho (\\nu )}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\rho(\\nu)" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/771818acdb33bf67ca39d6f197a871dae92752b3" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:4.243ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> of ambient photons of that frequency,\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle R_{ji}=N_{j}B_{ji}\\rho (\\nu )\\!}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>R</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>j</mi>\n<mi>i</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>N</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>j</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msub>\n<mi>B</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>j</mi>\n<mi>i</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mi>&#961;<!-- &#961; --></mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>&#957;<!-- &#957; --></mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mspace width="negativethinmathspace"></mspace>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle R_{ji}=N_{j}B_{ji}\\rho (\\nu )\\!}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\nR_{ji}=N_{j} B_{ji} \\rho(\\nu) \\!\n" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6c77cd3b1d4802a0110925a4cfe934b76a66a45b" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; margin-right: -0.166ex; width:16.379ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle B_{ji}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>B</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>j</mi>\n<mi>i</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle B_{ji}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="B_{ji}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/baf9e62f616a2934a55bccd69e744f38035d8b93" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:3.241ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rate_constant" title="Rate constant">rate constant</a> for absorption. For the reverse process, there are two possibilities: spontaneous emission of a photon, or the emission of a photon initiated by the interaction of the atom with a passing photon and the return of the atom to the lower-energy state. Following Einstein\'s approach, the corresponding rate <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle R_{ij}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>R</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n<mi>j</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle R_{ij}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="R_{ij}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/50382df1fd3bebf0baf22e2eab56a7a471d6621a" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:3.241ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> for the emission of photons of frequency <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\nu }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>&#957;<!-- &#957; --></mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\nu }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\nu " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c15bbbb971240cf328aba572178f091684585468" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.232ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> and transition from a higher energy <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle E_{i}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle E_{i}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="E_{i}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8ba9f6e3041b052cf13a0ede4ecf35fb4c9cd16c" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.515ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> to a lower energy <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle E_{j}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>j</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle E_{j}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="E_{j}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a01babf2293d37d9ff73b61202cfc3a3933eaf19" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:2.625ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> is\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle R_{ij}=N_{i}A_{ij}+N_{i}B_{ij}\\rho (\\nu )\\!}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>R</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n<mi>j</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>N</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msub>\n<mi>A</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n<mi>j</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>N</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msub>\n<mi>B</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n<mi>j</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mi>&#961;<!-- &#961; --></mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>&#957;<!-- &#957; --></mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mspace width="negativethinmathspace"></mspace>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle R_{ij}=N_{i}A_{ij}+N_{i}B_{ij}\\rho (\\nu )\\!}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\nR_{ij}=N_{i} A_{ij} + N_{i} B_{ij} \\rho(\\nu) \\!\n" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3715c7cb37eb0865f07655b7b0318c8308279369" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; margin-right: -0.166ex; width:24.996ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle A_{ij}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>A</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n<mi>j</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle A_{ij}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="A_{ij}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8272b28f5aae6dbb8d6f829d58bab353b21bde20" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:3.22ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> is the rate constant for <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_emission" title="Spontaneous emission">emitting a photon spontaneously</a>, and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle B_{ij}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>B</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n<mi>j</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle B_{ij}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="B_{ij}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/57c134504644caba8b83f479c9f27dbfa990b79a" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:3.241ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> is the rate constant for emissions in response to ambient photons (<a href="/wiki/Stimulated_emission" title="Stimulated emission">induced or stimulated emission</a>). In thermodynamic equilibrium, the number of atoms in state i and those in state j must, on average, be constant; hence, the rates <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle R_{ji}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>R</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>j</mi>\n<mi>i</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle R_{ji}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="R_{ji}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/07aa016887d6fd7c4d7f5041899cb34d8ba1154e" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:3.241ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle R_{ij}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>R</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n<mi>j</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle R_{ij}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="R_{ij}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/50382df1fd3bebf0baf22e2eab56a7a471d6621a" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:3.241ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> must be equal. Also, by arguments analogous to the derivation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boltzmann_statistics" title="Boltzmann statistics">Boltzmann statistics</a>, the ratio of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle N_{i}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>N</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle N_{i}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="N_{i}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/fef58cebf23adff9199f17325aefb5515fdca99d" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.666ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle N_{j}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>N</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>j</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle N_{j}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="N_{j}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e42efe207e17a74b4c17b1aa0cced7a84501bec9" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:2.776ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> is <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle g_{i}/g_{j}\\exp {(E_{j}-E_{i})/(kT)},}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>g</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<msub>\n<mi>g</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>j</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mi>exp</mi>\n<mo>&#8289;<!-- &#8289; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>j</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>k</mi>\n<mi>T</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mo>,</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle g_{i}/g_{j}\\exp {(E_{j}-E_{i})/(kT)},}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle g_{i}/g_{j}\\exp {(E_{j}-E_{i})/(kT)},}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/dffee654ba7c336692545142574aa3990632752b" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:25.672ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span> where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle g_{i,j}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>g</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n<mo>,</mo>\n<mi>j</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle g_{i,j}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="g_{i,j}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/cb2fe270b32fba161f2f6faf9e14938ece246ce7" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:3.044ex; height:2.343ex;"/></span> are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Degenerate_energy_level" title="Degenerate energy level">degeneracy</a> of the state i and that of j, respectively, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle E_{i,j}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n<mo>,</mo>\n<mi>j</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle E_{i,j}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="E_{i,j}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e7814421833a1b6fae2769bb795158a0c02ecda0" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:3.65ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> their energies, k the <a href="/wiki/Boltzmann_constant" title="Boltzmann constant">Boltzmann constant</a> and T the system\'s <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperature</a>. From this, it is readily derived that\n<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle g_{i}B_{ij}=g_{j}B_{ji}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>g</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msub>\n<mi>B</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n<mi>j</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>g</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>j</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msub>\n<mi>B</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>j</mi>\n<mi>i</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle g_{i}B_{ij}=g_{j}B_{ji}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="g_iB_{ij}=g_jB_{ji}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b05f1a0bd93673e8867ac6c3728a2fd60108f6bc" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:13.508ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> and\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle A_{ij}={\\frac {8\\pi h\\nu ^{3}}{c^{3}}}B_{ij}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>A</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n<mi>j</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mn>8</mn>\n<mi>&#960;<!-- &#960; --></mi>\n<mi>h</mi>\n<msup>\n<mi>&#957;<!-- &#957; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>3</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mrow>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>3</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<msub>\n<mi>B</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n<mi>j</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>.</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle A_{ij}={\\frac {8\\pi h\\nu ^{3}}{c^{3}}}B_{ij}.}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\nA_{ij}=\\frac{8 \\pi h \\nu^{3}}{c^{3}} B_{ij}.\n" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c9cc46d6112932e77fcb8f37f57214992420d544" style="vertical-align: -2.171ex; width:17.188ex; height:6.009ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>The A and Bs are collectively known as the <i>Einstein coefficients</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[80]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Einstein could not fully justify his rate equations, but claimed that it should be possible to calculate the coefficients <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle A_{ij}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>A</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n<mi>j</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle A_{ij}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="A_{ij}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8272b28f5aae6dbb8d6f829d58bab353b21bde20" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:3.22ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span>, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle B_{ji}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>B</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>j</mi>\n<mi>i</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle B_{ji}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="B_{ji}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/baf9e62f616a2934a55bccd69e744f38035d8b93" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:3.241ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle B_{ij}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>B</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n<mi>j</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle B_{ij}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="B_{ij}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/57c134504644caba8b83f479c9f27dbfa990b79a" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:3.241ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> once physicists had obtained "mechanics and electrodynamics modified to accommodate the quantum hypothesis".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[81]</a></sup> In fact, in 1926, <a href="/wiki/Paul_Dirac" title="Paul Dirac">Paul Dirac</a> derived the <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle B_{ij}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>B</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n<mi>j</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle B_{ij}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="B_{ij}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/57c134504644caba8b83f479c9f27dbfa990b79a" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:3.241ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> rate constants by using a semiclassical approach,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dirac1926_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dirac1926-87">[82]</a></sup> and, in 1927, succeeded in deriving <i>all</i> the rate constants from first principles within the framework of quantum theory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dirac1927a_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dirac1927a-88">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dirac1927b_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dirac1927b-89">[84]</a></sup> Dirac\'s work was the foundation of quantum electrodynamics, i.e., the quantization of the electromagnetic field itself. Dirac\'s approach is also called <i>second quantization</i> or <a href="/wiki/Quantum_field_theory" title="Quantum field theory">quantum field theory</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heisenberg1929_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heisenberg1929-90">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heisenberg1930_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heisenberg1930-91">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fermi1932_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fermi1932-92">[87]</a></sup> earlier quantum mechanical treatments only treat material particles as quantum mechanical, not the electromagnetic field.\n</p><p>Einstein was troubled by the fact that his theory seemed incomplete, since it did not determine the <i>direction</i> of a spontaneously emitted photon. A probabilistic nature of light-particle motion was first considered by <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Newton</a> in his treatment of <a href="/wiki/Birefringence" title="Birefringence">birefringence</a> and, more generally, of the splitting of light beams at interfaces into a transmitted beam and a reflected beam. Newton hypothesized that hidden variables in the light particle determined which of the two paths a single photon would take.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Newton1730_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Newton1730-44">[41]</a></sup> Similarly, Einstein hoped for a more complete theory that would leave nothing to chance, beginning his separation<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pais1982_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pais1982-56">[52]</a></sup> from quantum mechanics. Ironically, <a href="/wiki/Max_Born" title="Max Born">Max Born</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Probability_amplitude" title="Probability amplitude">probabilistic interpretation</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Wave_function" title="Wave function">wave function</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Born1926a_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Born1926a-93">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Born1926b_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Born1926b-94">[89]</a></sup> was inspired by Einstein\'s later work searching for a more complete theory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ghost_field_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-ghost_field-95">[90]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_quantization_and_high_energy_photon_interactions">Second quantization and high energy photon interactions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Second quantization and high energy photon interactions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Quantum_field_theory" title="Quantum field theory">Quantum field theory</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:VisibleEmrWavelengths.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="440" data-file-width="480" decoding="async" height="229" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/VisibleEmrWavelengths.svg/250px-VisibleEmrWavelengths.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/VisibleEmrWavelengths.svg/375px-VisibleEmrWavelengths.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/VisibleEmrWavelengths.svg/500px-VisibleEmrWavelengths.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:VisibleEmrWavelengths.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Different <i>electromagnetic modes</i> (such as those depicted here) can be treated as independent <a href="/wiki/Quantum_harmonic_oscillator" title="Quantum harmonic oscillator">simple harmonic oscillators</a>. A photon corresponds to a unit of energy E=h&#957; in its electromagnetic mode.</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1910, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Debye" title="Peter Debye">Peter Debye</a> derived <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planck%27s_law_of_black-body_radiation" title="Planck\'s law of black-body radiation">Planck\'s law of black-body radiation</a> from a relatively simple assumption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Debye1910_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Debye1910-96">[91]</a></sup> He correctly decomposed the electromagnetic field in a cavity into its <a href="/wiki/Fourier_series" title="Fourier series">Fourier modes</a>, and assumed that the energy in any mode was an integer multiple of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle h\\nu }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>h</mi>\n<mi>&#957;<!-- &#957; --></mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle h\\nu }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="h\\nu " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3cc23768f43b26085f80e1882a94b31d24abd653" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.571ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>, where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\nu }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>&#957;<!-- &#957; --></mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\nu }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\nu " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c15bbbb971240cf328aba572178f091684585468" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.232ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> is the frequency of the electromagnetic mode. Planck\'s law of black-body radiation follows immediately as a geometric sum. However, Debye\'s approach failed to give the correct formula for the energy fluctuations of blackbody radiation, which were derived by Einstein in 1909.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Einstein1909_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-Einstein1909-52">[48]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1925, <a href="/wiki/Max_Born" title="Max Born">Born</a>, <a href="/wiki/Werner_Heisenberg" title="Werner Heisenberg">Heisenberg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pascual_Jordan" title="Pascual Jordan">Jordan</a> reinterpreted Debye\'s concept in a key way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Born1925_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Born1925-97">[92]</a></sup> As may be shown classically, the <a href="/wiki/Fourier_series" title="Fourier series">Fourier modes</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_four-potential" title="Electromagnetic four-potential">electromagnetic field</a>&#8212;a complete set of electromagnetic plane waves indexed by their wave vector <b>k</b> and polarization state&#8212;are equivalent to a set of uncoupled <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simple_harmonic_oscillator" title="Simple harmonic oscillator">simple harmonic oscillators</a>. Treated quantum mechanically, the energy levels of such oscillators are known to be <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle E=nh\\nu }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi>n</mi>\n<mi>h</mi>\n<mi>&#957;<!-- &#957; --></mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle E=nh\\nu }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="E=nh\\nu" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b5a53b66a9439e07293b61135c915a7b19c33096" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:8.84ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>, where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\nu }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>&#957;<!-- &#957; --></mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\nu }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\nu " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c15bbbb971240cf328aba572178f091684585468" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.232ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> is the oscillator frequency. The key new step was to identify an electromagnetic mode with energy <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle E=nh\\nu }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi>n</mi>\n<mi>h</mi>\n<mi>&#957;<!-- &#957; --></mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle E=nh\\nu }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="E=nh\\nu" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b5a53b66a9439e07293b61135c915a7b19c33096" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:8.84ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> as a state with <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle n}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>n</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle n}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="n" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.395ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> photons, each of energy <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle h\\nu }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>h</mi>\n<mi>&#957;<!-- &#957; --></mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle h\\nu }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="h\\nu " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3cc23768f43b26085f80e1882a94b31d24abd653" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.571ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>. This approach gives the correct energy fluctuation formula.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vertex_correction.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="286" data-file-width="271" decoding="async" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Vertex_correction.svg/220px-Vertex_correction.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Vertex_correction.svg/330px-Vertex_correction.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Vertex_correction.svg/440px-Vertex_correction.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vertex_correction.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In quantum field theory, the probability of an event is computed by summing the <a href="/wiki/Probability_amplitude" title="Probability amplitude">probability amplitude</a> (a <a href="/wiki/Complex_number" title="Complex number">complex number</a>) for all possible ways in which the event can occur, as in the <a href="/wiki/Feynman_diagram" title="Feynman diagram">Feynman diagram</a> shown here; the probability equals the square of the <a href="/wiki/Absolute_value" title="Absolute value">modulus</a> of the total amplitude.</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Paul_Dirac" title="Paul Dirac">Dirac</a> took this one step further.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dirac1927a_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dirac1927a-88">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dirac1927b_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dirac1927b-89">[84]</a></sup> He treated the interaction between a charge and an electromagnetic field as a small perturbation that induces transitions in the photon states, changing the numbers of photons in the modes, while conserving energy and momentum overall. Dirac was able to derive Einstein\'s <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle A_{ij}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>A</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n<mi>j</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle A_{ij}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="A_{ij}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8272b28f5aae6dbb8d6f829d58bab353b21bde20" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:3.22ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle B_{ij}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>B</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n<mi>j</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle B_{ij}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="B_{ij}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/57c134504644caba8b83f479c9f27dbfa990b79a" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:3.241ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> coefficients from first principles, and showed that the Bose&#8211;Einstein statistics of photons is a natural consequence of quantizing the electromagnetic field correctly (Bose\'s reasoning went in the opposite direction; he derived <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planck%27s_law_of_black-body_radiation" title="Planck\'s law of black-body radiation">Planck\'s law of black-body radiation</a> by <i>assuming</i> B&#8211;E statistics). In Dirac\'s time, it was not yet known that all bosons, including photons, must obey Bose&#8211;Einstein statistics.\n</p><p>Dirac\'s second-order <a href="/wiki/Perturbation_theory_(quantum_mechanics)" title="Perturbation theory (quantum mechanics)">perturbation theory</a> can involve <a href="/wiki/Virtual_particle" title="Virtual particle">virtual photons</a>, transient intermediate states of the electromagnetic field; the static <a href="/wiki/Coulomb%27s_law" title="Coulomb\'s law">electric</a> and <a href="/wiki/Magnetism" title="Magnetism">magnetic</a> interactions are mediated by such virtual photons. In such <a href="/wiki/Quantum_field_theory" title="Quantum field theory">quantum field theories</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Probability_amplitude" title="Probability amplitude">probability amplitude</a> of observable events is calculated by summing over <i>all</i> possible intermediate steps, even ones that are unphysical; hence, virtual photons are not constrained to satisfy <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle E=pc}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi>p</mi>\n<mi>c</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle E=pc}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="E=pc" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5498982f39427c7573448da10de26a7b3209e47a" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:7.05ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>, and may have extra <a href="/wiki/Polarization_(waves)" title="Polarization (waves)">polarization</a> states; depending on the <a href="/wiki/Gauge_fixing" title="Gauge fixing">gauge</a> used, virtual photons may have three or four polarization states, instead of the two states of real photons. Although these transient virtual photons can never be observed, they contribute measurably to the probabilities of observable events. Indeed, such second-order and higher-order perturbation calculations can give apparently <a href="/wiki/Infinity" title="Infinity">infinite</a> contributions to the sum. Such unphysical results are corrected for using the technique of <a href="/wiki/Renormalization" title="Renormalization">renormalization</a>.\n</p><p>Other virtual particles may contribute to the summation as well; for example, two photons may interact indirectly through virtual <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electron</a>&#8211;<a href="/wiki/Positron" title="Positron">positron</a> <a href="/wiki/Pair_production" title="Pair production">pairs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[93]</a></sup> In fact, such photon&#8211;photon scattering (see <a href="/wiki/Two-photon_physics" title="Two-photon physics">two-photon physics</a>), as well as electron&#8211;photon scattering, is meant to be one of the modes of operations of the planned particle accelerator, the <a href="/wiki/International_Linear_Collider" title="International Linear Collider">International Linear Collider</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[94]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Modern_physics" title="Modern physics">modern physics</a> notation, the <a href="/wiki/Quantum_state" title="Quantum state">quantum state</a> of the electromagnetic field is written as a <a href="/wiki/Fock_state" title="Fock state">Fock state</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Tensor_product" title="Tensor product">tensor product</a> of the states for each electromagnetic mode\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle |n_{k_{0}}\\rangle \\otimes |n_{k_{1}}\\rangle \\otimes \\dots \\otimes |n_{k_{n}}\\rangle \\dots }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo stretchy="false">|</mo>\n</mrow>\n<msub>\n<mi>n</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<msub>\n<mi>k</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">&#10217;<!-- &#10217; --></mo>\n<mo>&#8855;<!-- &#8855; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo stretchy="false">|</mo>\n</mrow>\n<msub>\n<mi>n</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<msub>\n<mi>k</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">&#10217;<!-- &#10217; --></mo>\n<mo>&#8855;<!-- &#8855; --></mo>\n<mo>&#8943;<!-- &#8943; --></mo>\n<mo>&#8855;<!-- &#8855; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo stretchy="false">|</mo>\n</mrow>\n<msub>\n<mi>n</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<msub>\n<mi>k</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>n</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">&#10217;<!-- &#10217; --></mo>\n<mo>&#8230;<!-- &#8230; --></mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle |n_{k_{0}}\\rangle \\otimes |n_{k_{1}}\\rangle \\otimes \\dots \\otimes |n_{k_{n}}\\rangle \\dots }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="|n_{k_0}\\rangle\\otimes|n_{k_1}\\rangle\\otimes\\dots\\otimes|n_{k_n}\\rangle\\dots" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1bf1d8ffa71f6f78681ad32f6ff5870627b1cd79" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:29.088ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle |n_{k_{i}}\\rangle }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo stretchy="false">|</mo>\n</mrow>\n<msub>\n<mi>n</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<msub>\n<mi>k</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">&#10217;<!-- &#10217; --></mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle |n_{k_{i}}\\rangle }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="|n_{k_i}\\rangle" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/261582c861f317f3e8cd9e33c36c8d10e0d62a49" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:4.66ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span> represents the state in which <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\,n_{k_{i}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>\n<msub>\n<mi>n</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<msub>\n<mi>k</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\,n_{k_{i}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\, n_{k_i}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a6945fb83c5b12e2c726c9a26195feb2207b0d1d" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:3.495ex; height:2.343ex;"/></span> photons are in the mode <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle k_{i}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>k</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle k_{i}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="k_{i}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f29138ed3ad54ffce527daccadc49c520459b0b0" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.011ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>. In this notation, the creation of a new photon in mode <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle k_{i}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>k</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle k_{i}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="k_{i}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f29138ed3ad54ffce527daccadc49c520459b0b0" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.011ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> (e.g., emitted from an atomic transition) is written as <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle |n_{k_{i}}\\rangle \\rightarrow |n_{k_{i}}+1\\rangle }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo stretchy="false">|</mo>\n</mrow>\n<msub>\n<mi>n</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<msub>\n<mi>k</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">&#10217;<!-- &#10217; --></mo>\n<mo stretchy="false">&#8594;<!-- &#8594; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo stretchy="false">|</mo>\n</mrow>\n<msub>\n<mi>n</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<msub>\n<mi>k</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">&#10217;<!-- &#10217; --></mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle |n_{k_{i}}\\rangle \\rightarrow |n_{k_{i}}+1\\rangle }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="|n_{k_i}\\rangle \\rightarrow|n_{k_i}+1\\rangle" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/98ad7c2b75079bb41e6e1719263ff9fc799e8161" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:16.937ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span>. This notation merely expresses the concept of Born, Heisenberg and Jordan described above, and does not add any physics.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="The_hadronic_properties_of_the_photon">The hadronic properties of the photon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: The hadronic properties of the photon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Measurements of the interaction between energetic photons and <a href="/wiki/Hadron" title="Hadron">hadrons</a> show that the interaction is much more intense than expected by the interaction of merely photons with the hadron\'s electric charge. Furthermore, the interaction of energetic photons with protons is similar to the interaction of photons with neutrons<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[95]</a></sup> in spite of the fact that the electric charge structures of protons and neutrons are substantially different. A theory called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vector_Meson_Dominance" title="Vector Meson Dominance">Vector Meson Dominance</a> (VMD) was developed to explain this effect. According to VMD, the photon is a superposition of the pure electromagnetic photon which interacts only with electric charges and vector mesons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[96]</a></sup> However, if experimentally probed at very short distances, the intrinsic structure of the photon is recognized as a flux of quark and gluon components, quasi-free according to asymptotic freedom in <a href="/wiki/Quantum_chromodynamics" title="Quantum chromodynamics">QCD</a> and described by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photon_Structure_Function" title="Photon Structure Function">photon structure function</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[98]</a></sup> A comprehensive comparison of data with theoretical predictions was presented in a review in 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[99]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="The_photon_as_a_gauge_boson">The photon as a gauge boson</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: The photon as a gauge boson">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gauge_theory" title="Gauge theory">Gauge theory</a></div>\n<p>The electromagnetic field can be understood as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gauge_field" title="Gauge field">gauge field</a>, i.e., as a field that results from requiring that a gauge symmetry holds independently at every position in <a href="/wiki/Spacetime" title="Spacetime">spacetime</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ryder_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ryder-105">[100]</a></sup> For the <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_field" title="Electromagnetic field">electromagnetic field</a>, this gauge symmetry is the <a href="/wiki/Abelian_group" title="Abelian group">Abelian</a> <a href="/wiki/Unitary_group" title="Unitary group">U(1) symmetry</a> of <a href="/wiki/Complex_number" title="Complex number">complex numbers</a> of absolute value 1, which reflects the ability to vary the <a href="/wiki/Complex_geometry" title="Complex geometry">phase</a> of a complex field without affecting <a href="/wiki/Observable" title="Observable">observables</a> or <a href="/wiki/Real_number" title="Real number">real valued functions</a> made from it, such as the <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Lagrangian_(field_theory)" title="Lagrangian (field theory)">Lagrangian</a>.\n</p><p>The quanta of an <a href="/wiki/Gauge_theory" title="Gauge theory">Abelian gauge field</a> must be massless, uncharged bosons, as long as the symmetry is not broken; hence, the photon is predicted to be massless, and to have zero <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">electric charge</a> and integer spin. The particular form of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_interaction" title="Electromagnetic interaction">electromagnetic interaction</a> specifies that the photon must have <a href="/wiki/Spin_(physics)" title="Spin (physics)">spin</a> &#177;1; thus, its <a href="/wiki/Helicity_(particle_physics)" title="Helicity (particle physics)">helicity</a> must be <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\pm \\hbar }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mo>&#177;<!-- &#177; --></mo>\n<mi class="MJX-variant">&#8463;<!-- &#8463; --></mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\pm \\hbar }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\pm \\hbar" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5c578ae2b02e515c92a2981d6db0ffb5943e3357" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:3.115ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>. These two spin components correspond to the classical concepts of <a href="/wiki/Circular_polarization" title="Circular polarization">right-handed and left-handed circularly polarized</a> light. However, the transient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Virtual_photon" title="Virtual photon">virtual photons</a> of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_electrodynamics" title="Quantum electrodynamics">quantum electrodynamics</a> may also adopt unphysical polarization states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ryder_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ryder-105">[100]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the prevailing <a href="/wiki/Standard_Model" title="Standard Model">Standard Model</a> of physics, the photon is one of four <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gauge_bosons" title="Gauge bosons">gauge bosons</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Electroweak_interaction" title="Electroweak interaction">electroweak interaction</a>; the <a href="/wiki/W_and_Z_bosons" title="W and Z bosons">other three</a> are denoted W<sup>+</sup>, W<sup>&#8722;</sup> and Z<sup>0</sup> and are responsible for the <a href="/wiki/Weak_interaction" title="Weak interaction">weak interaction</a>. Unlike the photon, these gauge bosons have <a href="/wiki/Invariant_mass" title="Invariant mass">mass</a>, owing to a <a href="/wiki/Higgs_mechanism" title="Higgs mechanism">mechanism</a> that breaks their <a href="/wiki/Special_unitary_group" title="Special unitary group">SU(2) gauge symmetry</a>. The unification of the photon with W and Z gauge bosons in the electroweak interaction was accomplished by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sheldon_Glashow" title="Sheldon Glashow">Sheldon Glashow</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abdus_Salam" title="Abdus Salam">Abdus Salam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Steven_Weinberg" title="Steven Weinberg">Steven Weinberg</a>, for which they were awarded the 1979 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize" title="Nobel Prize">Nobel Prize</a> in physics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glashow_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Glashow-106">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salam_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Salam-107">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weinberg_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weinberg-108">[103]</a></sup> Physicists continue to hypothesize <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grand_unification_theory" title="Grand unification theory">grand unified theories</a> that connect these four <a href="/wiki/Gauge_boson" title="Gauge boson">gauge bosons</a> with the eight <a href="/wiki/Gluon" title="Gluon">gluon</a> gauge bosons of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_chromodynamics" title="Quantum chromodynamics">quantum chromodynamics</a>; however, key predictions of these theories, such as <a href="/wiki/Proton_decay" title="Proton decay">proton decay</a>, have not been observed experimentally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[104]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Contributions_to_the_mass_of_a_system">Contributions to the mass of a system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Contributions to the mass of a system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Mass_in_special_relativity" title="Mass in special relativity">Mass in special relativity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mass_in_general_relativity" title="Mass in general relativity">Mass in general relativity</a></div>\n<p>The energy of a system that emits a photon is <i>decreased</i> by the energy <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle E}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>E</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle E}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="E" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4232c9de2ee3eec0a9c0a19b15ab92daa6223f9b" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.776ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> of the photon as measured in the rest frame of the emitting system, which may result in a reduction in mass in the amount <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle {E}/{c^{2}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>E</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle {E}/{c^{2}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{E}/{c^2}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/61f55812ff776dde9279b91f06e7993ddff94932" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:4.999ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span>. Similarly, the mass of a system that absorbs a photon is <i>increased</i> by a corresponding amount. As an application, the energy balance of nuclear reactions involving photons is commonly written in terms of the masses of the nuclei involved, and terms of the form <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle {E}/{c^{2}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>E</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle {E}/{c^{2}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{E}/{c^2}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/61f55812ff776dde9279b91f06e7993ddff94932" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:4.999ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span> for the gamma photons (and for other relevant energies, such as the recoil energy of nuclei).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[105]</a></sup>\n</p><p>This concept is applied in key predictions of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_electrodynamics" title="Quantum electrodynamics">quantum electrodynamics</a> (QED, see above). In that theory, the mass of electrons (or, more generally, leptons) is modified by including the mass contributions of virtual photons, in a technique known as <a href="/wiki/Renormalization" title="Renormalization">renormalization</a>. Such "<a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Radiative_correction&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Radiative correction (page does not exist)">radiative corrections</a>" contribute to a number of predictions of QED, such as the <a href="/wiki/Anomalous_magnetic_dipole_moment" title="Anomalous magnetic dipole moment">magnetic dipole moment</a> of <a href="/wiki/Lepton" title="Lepton">leptons</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Lamb_shift" title="Lamb shift">Lamb shift</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Hyperfine_structure" title="Hyperfine structure">hyperfine structure</a> of bound lepton pairs, such as <a href="/wiki/Muonium" title="Muonium">muonium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Positronium" title="Positronium">positronium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[106]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Since photons contribute to the <a href="/wiki/Stress%E2%80%93energy_tensor" title="Stress&#8211;energy tensor">stress&#8211;energy tensor</a>, they exert a <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravitational attraction</a> on other objects, according to the theory of <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a>. Conversely, photons are themselves affected by gravity; their normally straight trajectories may be bent by warped <a href="/wiki/Spacetime" title="Spacetime">spacetime</a>, as in <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_lens" title="Gravitational lens">gravitational lensing</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_redshift" title="Gravitational redshift">their frequencies may be lowered</a> by moving to a higher <a href="/wiki/Potential_energy" title="Potential energy">gravitational potential</a>, as in the <a href="/wiki/Pound%E2%80%93Rebka_experiment" title="Pound&#8211;Rebka experiment">Pound&#8211;Rebka experiment</a>. However, these effects are not specific to photons; exactly the same effects would be predicted for classical <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic waves</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[107]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Photons_in_matter">Photons in matter</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Photons in matter">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Group_velocity" title="Group velocity">Group velocity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Photochemistry" title="Photochemistry">Photochemistry</a></div>\n<p>Light that travels through transparent matter does so at a lower speed than <i>c</i>, the speed of light in a vacuum. For example, photons engage in so many collisions on the way from the <a href="/wiki/Solar_core" title="Solar core">core</a> of the sun that radiant energy can take about a million years to reach the surface;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[108]</a></sup> however, once in open space, a photon takes only 8.3 minutes to reach Earth. The factor by which the speed is decreased is called the <a href="/wiki/Refractive_index" title="Refractive index">refractive index</a> of the material. In a classical wave picture, the slowing can be explained by the light inducing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_polarization" title="Electric polarization">electric polarization</a> in the matter, the polarized matter radiating new light, and that new light interfering with the original light wave to form a delayed wave. In a particle picture, the slowing can instead be described as a blending of the photon with quantum excitations of the matter to produce <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quasi-particle" title="Quasi-particle">quasi-particles</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Polariton" title="Polariton">polariton</a> (other quasi-particles are <a href="/wiki/Phonon" title="Phonon">phonons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Exciton" title="Exciton">excitons</a>); this polariton has a nonzero <a href="/wiki/Effective_mass_(solid-state_physics)" title="Effective mass (solid-state physics)">effective mass</a>, which means that it cannot travel at <i>c</i>. Light of different frequencies may travel through matter at <a href="/wiki/Variable_speed_of_light" title="Variable speed of light">different speeds</a>; this is called <a href="/wiki/Dispersion_(optics)" title="Dispersion (optics)">dispersion</a> (not to be confused with scattering). In some cases, it can result in <a href="/wiki/Slow_light" title="Slow light">extremely slow speeds of light</a> in matter.
